   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Freedom in the World</i></h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div>

         <center>
          <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"><span class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#003399; color:white;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;</span> Countries designated "<a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">electoral democracies</a>" in Freedom House's <i>Freedom in the World 2020</i> survey, covering the year 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
         </center>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><i><b>Freedom in the World</b></i> is a yearly survey and report by the U.S.-based<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and partially <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">U.S. government</a> funded<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organization</a> <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a> that measures the degree of <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Political rights">political rights</a> in every nation and significant related and disputed territories around the world. </p> 
      <div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading">
       <input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_use">Origin and use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Freedom_in_the_World&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origin and use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p><i>Freedom in the World</i> was launched in 1973 by <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Gastil" title="Raymond Gastil">Raymond Gastil</a>. It produces annual scores representing the levels of political rights and civil liberties in each state and territory, on a scale from 1 (most free) to 7 (least free). Depending on the ratings, the nations are then classified as "Free", "Part(ial)ly Free", or "Not Free".<sup id="cite_ref-Bollen_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bollen-5">[5]</a></sup> The report is often used by researchers in order to measure democracy and correlates highly with several other measures of democracy such as the <a href="/wiki/Polity_data_series" title="Polity data series">Polity data series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Freedom House rankings are widely reported in the media and used as sources by political researchers. Their construction and use has been <a href="#Criticism">evaluated by critics and supporters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AP03_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP03-7">[7]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Country_rankings">Country rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Freedom_in_the_World&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Country rankings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The rankings are from the <i>Freedom in the World</i> 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-FITW-TG-2015_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FITW-TG-2015-8">[8]</a></sup> 2016,<sup id="cite_ref-FITW-TG-2016_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FITW-TG-2016-9">[9]</a></sup> 2017,<sup id="cite_ref-FITW-TG-2017_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FITW-TG-2017-10">[10]</a></sup> 2018,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and 2019<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> surveys, each report covering the previous year. The average of each pair of ratings on political rights and civil liberties determines the overall status of "Free" (1.0–2.5), "Part(ial)ly Free" (3.0–5.0), or "Not Free" (5.5–7.0).<sup id="cite_ref-FITW-Methods-2010_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FITW-Methods-2010-13">[13]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>An asterisk (*) indicates countries which are "<a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">electoral democracies</a>". To qualify as an "electoral democracy", a state must have satisfied the following criteria: </p> 
      <ol>
       <li>A competitive, multiparty political system;</li> 
       <li>Adult suffrage for all citizens without criminal convictions (some states may further punish and subjugate people with criminal convictions by disenfranchising them from the democratic process);</li> 
       <li>Regularly contested elections conducted in conditions of ballot secrecy, reasonable ballot security, and the absence of massive voter fraud that yields results that are unrepresentative of the public will; and</li> 
       <li>Significant public access of major political parties to the electorate through the media and through generally open political campaigning.</li>
      </ol> 
      <p>An electoral democracy must have a score of 7 or more out of 12 in political rights subcategory A (Electoral Progress), an overall aggregate score of 20 in their political rights rating and an overall aggregate score of 30 in their civil liberties rating.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Freedom House's term "electoral democracy" differs from "<a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a>" in that the latter also implies the presence of a substantial array of civil liberties. In the survey, all Free countries qualify as both electoral and liberal democracies. By contrast, some Partly Free countries qualify as electoral, but not liberal, democracies.<sup id="cite_ref-FITW-Methods-2010_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FITW-Methods-2010-13">[13]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World">World</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Freedom_in_the_World&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: World">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>*</b>: Electoral democracies (as described above), <b>PR</b>: Political Rights, <b>CL</b>: Civil Liberties

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trends">Trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Freedom_in_the_World&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <img alt="Ambox current red Americas.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" decoding="async" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290">
          </div></td>
         <td class="mbox-text">
          <div class="mbox-text-span">

          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>According to Freedom House, a quarter of all declines of freedom in the world in 2016 took place in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> </p>

      <p><span class="anchor" id="Criticism"></span> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evaluation">Evaluation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Freedom_in_the_World&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Evaluation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House#Criticism" title="Freedom House">Freedom House §&nbsp;Criticism</a>
      </div> 
      <p>There is some debate over the neutrality of Freedom House and the methodology used for the <i>Freedom in the World</i> report, which has been written by <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Gastil" title="Raymond Gastil">Raymond&nbsp;D. Gastil</a> and his colleagues.<sup id="cite_ref-Bollen_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bollen-5">[5]</a></sup> The neutrality and biases of human-rights indices have been discussed in several publications by Kenneth A. Bollen.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Bollen wrote that "Considered together these criticisms suggest that some nations may have been incorrectly rated on Gastil's measures. However, none of the criticisms have demonstrated a systematic bias in all the ratings. Most of the evidence consists of anecdotal evidence of relatively few cases. Whether there is a systematic or sporadic slant in Gastil's ratings is an open question" (Bollen, 1986, p.&nbsp;586).<sup id="cite_ref-Bollen_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bollen-5">[5]</a></sup> The freedom index of <i>Freedom in the World</i> has a very strong and positive (at least an 80%) <a href="/wiki/Correlation" class="mw-redirect" title="Correlation">correlation</a> with three other democracy-indices studied in Mainwaring (2001, p.&nbsp;53).<sup id="cite_ref-springerlink.com_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-springerlink.com-21">[21]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideological_bias_or_neutrality">Ideological bias or neutrality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Freedom_in_the_World&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ideological bias or neutrality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In his 1986 study, Bollen discussed reviews of measurements of <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>, including the index reported in <i>Freedom in the World</i> (Bollen, 1986, p.&nbsp;585). Criticisms of <i>Freedom in the World</i> during the 1980s were discussed by Gastil (1990), who stated that "generally such criticism is based on opinions about Freedom House rather than detailed examination of survey ratings", a conclusion disputed by Giannone.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The definition of Freedom in Gastil (1982) and Freedom House (1990) emphasized liberties rather than the exercise of freedom, according to <a href="/wiki/Adam_Przeworski" title="Adam Przeworski">Adam Przeworski</a>, who gave the following example: In the United States, citizens are free to form political parties and to vote, yet even in presidential elections only half of U.S. "citizens" vote; in the U.S., "the same two parties speak in a commercially sponsored unison", wrote <a href="#CITEREFPrzeworski2003">Przeworski (2003</a>, p.&nbsp;277).<sup id="cite_ref-AP03_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP03-7">[7]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>More recent charges of ideological bias prompted Freedom House to issue this 2010 statement: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Freedom House does not maintain a culture-bound view of freedom. The methodology of the survey is grounded in basic standards of political rights and civil liberties, derived in large measure from relevant portions of the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>. These standards apply to all countries and territories, irrespective of geographical location, ethnic or religious composition, or level of economic development.<sup id="cite_ref-FITW-Methods-2010_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FITW-Methods-2010-13">[13]</a></sup> </p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Mainwaring et alia (2001, p.&nbsp;52)<sup id="cite_ref-springerlink.com_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-springerlink.com-21">[21]</a></sup> wrote that Freedom House's index had "two systematic biases: scores for leftist were tainted by political considerations, and changes in scores are sometimes driven by changes in their criteria rather than changes in real conditions." Nonetheless, when evaluated in Latin American countries yearly, Freedom House's index was very strongly and positively correlated with the index of <a href="/wiki/Adam_Przeworski" title="Adam Przeworski">Adam Przeworski</a> and with the index of the authors themselves: They evaluated <a href="/wiki/Pearson%27s_correlation_coefficient" class="mw-redirect" title="Pearson's correlation coefficient">Pearson's coefficient of linear correlation</a> between their index and Freedom&nbsp;House's index, which was 0.82; among these indices and the two others studied, the correlations were all between 0.80 and 0.86 (Mainwaring et alia, 2001, p.&nbsp;53).<sup id="cite_ref-springerlink.com_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-springerlink.com-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As previously quoted, Bollen criticized previous studies of <i>Freedom in the World</i> as anecdotal and inconclusive; they raised issues needing further study by scientific methods rather than anecdotes.<sup id="cite_ref-Bollen_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bollen-5">[5]</a></sup> Bollen studied the question of ideological bias using <a href="/wiki/Multivariate_statistics" title="Multivariate statistics">multivariate statistics</a>. Using their <a href="/wiki/Factor_analysis" title="Factor analysis">factor-analytic</a> <a href="/wiki/Statistical_model" title="Statistical model">model</a> for human-rights measurements, Bollen and Paxton estimate that Gastil's method produces a bias of -0.38&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard&nbsp;deviations</a>&nbsp;(s.d.) against <a href="/wiki/Marxist%E2%80%93Leninist" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxist–Leninist">Marxist–Leninist</a> countries and a larger bias, +0.5&nbsp;s.d., favoring Christian countries; similar results held for the methodology of Sussman (Bollen and Paxton, 2000, p.&nbsp;585).<sup id="cite_ref-BP_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-23">[23]</a></sup> In contrast, another method by a critic of <i>Freedom in the World</i> produced a bias for Leftist countries during the 1980s of at least +0.8&nbsp;s.d., a bias that is "consistent with the general finding that political scientists are more favorable to leftist politics than is the general population" (Bollen and Paxton, p.&nbsp;585).<sup id="cite_ref-BP_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-23">[23]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_and_conceptual_analysis">Use and conceptual analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Freedom_in_the_World&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Use and conceptual analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Criticisms of the reception and uses of the <i>Freedom in the World</i> report have been noted by Diego Giannone:<sup id="cite_ref-Giannone_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giannone-24">[24]</a></sup> </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>"Conceptual stretching", <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Sartori" title="Giovanni Sartori">Giovanni Sartori</a>'s critical term for a methodological shortcoming common in social studies.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Giannone reports as an example that, according to Landman and Hausermann (2003), "the index by FH has been used as a tool for measuring democracy, good governance, and human rights, thus producing a conceptual stretching which is a major cause of 'losses in connotative precision': in short, an instrument used to measure everything, in the end, is not able to discriminate against anything."<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>Issues with aggregation. Giannone quotes Scoble and Wiseberg's conclusion (1981) that "the sum of a civil liberty score of 4 and a political liberty score of 2 is the same as the sum of a civil liberty score of 2 and a political liberty score of 4 even though the substantive interpretation of these different combinations is different."<sup id="cite_ref-Scoble69_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scoble69-27">[27]</a></sup></li> 
       <li>"Lack of specificity and rigorousness in construction" and "inadequate level of transparency and replicability of the scales", the first referencing to Scoble <i>et alie</i> (1981) and the latter to Hadenius and Teorell (2005).<sup id="cite_ref-Giannone69_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giannone69-28">[28]</a></sup> In support of the latter, he also quotes the conclusion of Munck and Verkuilen (2002) that "the aggregate data offered by Freedom House has to be accepted largely on faith",<sup id="cite_ref-Munck_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munck-29">[29]</a></sup> due to the factors that "no set of coding rules is provided, and the sources of information are not identified with enough precision, and because disaggregated data have not been made available to independent scholars".<sup id="cite_ref-Giannone69_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giannone69-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_series">Time series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Freedom_in_the_World&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Time series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Panel_study" class="mw-redirect" title="Panel study">Panel study</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time_series" title="Time series">Time series</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In "Political and ideological aspects in the measurement of democracy: the Freedom House case" (2010) which reviewed changes to the methodology since 1990, Diego Giannone concluded that "because of the changes in methodology over time and the strict interconnection between methodological and political aspects, the FH data do not offer an unbroken and politically neutral time series, such that they should not be used for cross-time analyses even for the development of first hypotheses. The internal consistency of the data series is open to question."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On this topic, the Freedom House website replies that they have "made a number of modest methodological changes to adapt to evolving ideas about political rights and civil liberties. At the same time, the time series data are not revised retroactively, and any changes to the methodology are introduced incrementally in order to ensure the comparability of the ratings from year to year."<sup id="cite_ref-FITW-Methods-2010_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FITW-Methods-2010-13">[13]</a></sup> </p>

        <li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://freedomhouse.org/report/freedom-world/freedom-world-2018"><i>Freedom In the World 2018</i></a>, Freedom House.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bollen has held chairs as a Distinguished Professor of Sociology and the Director of the Howard W. Odum Institute for Research in Social Science at the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill (UNC-CH). Also serving as an Adjunct Professor of Statistics at UNC-CH, Bollen wrote the leading graduate textbook in <a href="/wiki/Structural_equation_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Structural equation model">structural equation models</a> (SEM), often called <a href="/wiki/LISREL" title="LISREL">LISREL</a> models; SEM modeling allows the summary of a large number of measurements using a small number of meaningful <a href="/wiki/Factor_analysis" title="Factor analysis">factors</a>. SEM was used by Bollen in the studies reported hereafter.</span> </li>
